metrics",{"loggedUserInfo":-1},{"currentArticle":536,"isPreviewPage":367,"hasSupplementalData":367,"showCrossmarkWidget":13,"articleTemplate":670,"currentArticlePageMetaInfo":671,"xmlParsedArticleContent":-1,"xmlParsingError":-1},{"id":537,"doi":538,"title":539,"acceptanceDate":540,"receptionDate":541,"publicationDate":542,"lastModifiedDate":543,"isPublished":13,"abstract":544,"researchTopic":545,"articleType":551,"stage":554,"keywords":556,"authors":565,"editors":596,"reviewers":606,"journal":624,"section":631,"impactMetrics":633,"volume":637,"articleVolume":638,"relatedArticles":639,"isPublishedV2":367,"contents":640,"files":644},732344,"10.3389/fpls.2021.732344","Variables Affecting Shoot Growth and Plantlet Recovery in Tissue Cultures of Drug-Type Cannabis sativa L.",["Date","2021-08-10T08:27:06.000Z"],["Date","2021-06-28T23:03:29.000Z"],["Date","2021-09-21T00:00:00.000Z"],["Date","2025-11-14T02:09:57.375Z"],"Tissue culture approaches are widely used in crop plants for the purposes of micropropagation, regeneration of plants through organogenesis, obtaining pathogen-free plantlets from meristem culture, and developing genetically modified plants. In the present research, we evaluated the variables that can influence the success of shoot growth plantlet production from tissue cultures of Cannabis sativa L. (marijuana). Various sterilization methods were tested to ensure shoot development from nodal explants by limiting the frequency of contaminating endophytes, which otherwise caused death of explants. Seven commercially grown THC-containing cannabis strains showed significant differences in response to shoot growth from nodal explants and from meristems on Murashige and Skoog (MS) medium containing thidiazuron (1M) and naphthaleneacetic acid (0.5 M) plus 1% activated charcoal. The effect of DWK or MS salts in media on shoot length and leaf numbers from nodal explants was compared and showed strain dependency with regards to the growth response. To obtain rooted plantlets, shoots from nodal and meristem explants of strain Moby Dick were evaluated for rooting following addition of sodium metasilicate, silver nitrate, IBA, kinetin, or 2,4-D. Sodium metasilicate improved the visual appearance of the foliage, and improved the rate of rooting. Silver nitrate also promoted rooting. Following acclimatization, plantlet recovery using hydroponic culture, peat plugs, and rockwool substrate was 57, 76 and 83%, respectively. The development of plantlets from meristems is described for the first time in C. sativa and has potential for obtaining pathogen-free plants. The response of leaf explants to callus formation in 11 strains on MS medium without activated charcoal was 35 to 100%, depending on the strain; organogenesis was not observed. The success in recovery of plantlets from nodal and meristematic explants is influenced by the cannabis strain and the degree of endophytic contamination of the explants, as well as frequency of rooting. The procedures described here have potential applications for research and commercial utility to obtain plantlets from tissue cultures of C. sativa.",{"id":546,"title":547,"articlesCount":548,"isMagazinePage":13,"slug":549,"isOpenForSubmission":367,"views":550},16712,"Behind the Smoke and Mirrors: Reflections on Improving Cannabis Production and Investigating Medical Potential",16,"behind-the-smoke-and-mirrors-reflections-on-improving-cannabis-production-and-investigating-medical-potential",472870,{"id":552,"name":553},24,"Original Research",{"id":555,"name":19},18,[557,558,559,560,561,562,563,564],"meristems","Nodal explants","Shoot Growth","Rooting","plantlet recovery","Micropropagation","Callogenesis","Cannabis sativa L",[566,575,582,589],{"id":567,"firstName":568,"middleName":19,"lastName":569,"givenNames":570,"isCorresponding":367,"isProfilePublic":13,"userId":567,"email":-1,"affiliations":571},669272,"Janesse E.","Holmes","Janesse E. Plant Sci.","1664-462X",{"id":628,"domainId":392},66,[630],{"section":631},{"id":124,"name":125,"slug":126,"specialtyId":632},1812,{"views":634,"downloads":635,"citations":636},23059,4799,32,12,"Volume 12 - 2021",[],{"titleHtml":641,"fullTextHtml":642,"menuHtml":643},"Variables Affecting Shoot Growth and Plantlet Recovery in Tissue Cultures of Drug-Type \u003Ci>Cannabis sativa\u003C/i> L.","\u003Cdiv class=\"JournalAbstract\">\n\u003Ca id=\"h1\" name=\"h1\">\u003C/a>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"authors\">\u003Cspan class=\"author-wrapper notranslate\">\n\u003Ca href=\"https://loop.frontiersin.org/people/669272\" class=\"user-id-669272\">\u003Cimg class=\"pr5\" src=\"https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/669272/74\" onerror=\"this.onerror=null;this.src='https://loop.frontiersin.org/cdn/images/profile/default_32.jpg';\" alt=\"\nJanesse E. Holmes\">Janesse E. Holmes\u003C/a>\u003C/span>\u003Cspan class=\"author-wrapper notranslate\">\u003Ca href=\"https://loop.frontiersin.org/people/1447435\" class=\"user-id-1447435\">\u003Cimg class=\"pr5\" src=\"https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/1447435/74\" onerror=\"this.onerror=null;this.src='https://loop.frontiersin.org/cdn/images/profile/default_32.jpg';\" alt=\"Samantha Lung\">Samantha Lung\u003C/a>\u003C/span>\u003Cspan class=\"author-wrapper notranslate\">\u003Ca href=\"https://loop.frontiersin.org/people/656208\" class=\"user-id-656208\">\u003Cimg class=\"pr5\" src=\"https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/656208/74\" onerror=\"this.onerror=null;this.src='https://loop.frontiersin.org/cdn/images/profile/default_32.jpg';\" alt=\"Danielle Collyer\">Danielle Collyer\u003C/a>\u003C/span>\u003Cspan class=\"author-wrapper notranslate\">\u003Ca href=\"https://loop.frontiersin.org/people/585479\" class=\"user-id-585479\">\u003Cimg class=\"pr5\" src=\"https://loop.frontiersin.org/images/profile/585479/74\" onerror=\"this.onerror=null;this.src='https://loop.frontiersin.org/cdn/images/profile/default_32.jpg';\" alt=\"Zamir K. Punja&#xA;\">Zamir K. Punja\u003C/a>\u003Csup>&#x0002A;\u003C/sup>\u003C/span>\u003C/div>\n\u003Cul class=\"notes\">\u003Cli class=\"pl0\">Department of Biological Sciences, Simon Fraser University, Burnaby, BC, Canada\u003C/li>\n\u003C/ul>\n\u003Cp>Tissue culture approaches are widely used in crop plants for the purposes of micropropagation, regeneration of plants through organogenesis, obtaining pathogen-free plantlets from meristem culture, and developing genetically modified plants. In this research, we evaluated variables that can influence the success of shoot growth and plantlet production in tissue cultures of drug-type \u003Ci>Cannabis sativa\u003C/i> L. (marijuana). Various sterilization methods were tested to ensure shoot development from nodal explants by limiting the frequency of contaminating endophytes, which otherwise caused the death of explants. Seven commercially grown tetrahydrocannabinol (THC)-containing cannabis genotypes (strains) showed significant differences in response to shoot growth from meristems and nodal explants on Murashige and Skoog (MS) medium containing thidiazuron (1 &#x003BC;M) and naphthaleneacetic acid (0.5 &#x003BC;M) plus 1% activated charcoal. The effect of Driver and Kuniyuki Walnut (DKW) or MS basal salts in media on shoot length and leaf numbers from nodal explants was compared and showed genotype dependency with regard to the growth response. To obtain rooted plantlets, shoots from meristems and nodal explants of genotype Moby Dick were evaluated for rooting, following the addition of sodium metasilicate, silver nitrate, indole-3-butyric acid (IBA), kinetin, or 2,4-D. Sodium metasilicate improved the visual appearance of the foliage and improved the rate of rooting. Silver nitrate also promoted rooting. Following acclimatization, plantlet survival in hydroponic culture, peat plugs, and rockwool substrate was 57, 76, and 83%, respectively. The development of plantlets from meristems is described for the first time in \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> and has potential for obtaining pathogen-free plants. The callogenesis response of leaf explants of 11 genotypes on MS medium without activated charcoal was 35% to 100%, depending on the genotype; organogenesis was not observed. The success in recovery of plantlets from meristems and nodal explants is influenced by cannabis genotype, degree of endophytic contamination of the explants, and frequency of rooting. The procedures described here have potential applications for research and commercial utility to obtain plantlets in stage 1 tissue cultures of \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i>.\u003C/p>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\n\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"JournalFullText\">\n\u003Ca id=\"h2\" name=\"h2\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Introduction\u003C/h2>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">\u003Ci>Cannabis sativa\u003C/i> L., a member of the Cannabaceae family, is a dioecious, annual flowering plant that has been cultivated for thousands of years for its fiber (as hemp) and medicinal and psychotropic properties (as cannabis or marijuana). Vegetative cuttings are the conventional method for commercial propagation of cannabis to ensure rapid propagation of desired genotypes without the introduction of genetic variability resulting from sexual reproduction as \u003Ci>C. sativa\u003C/i> is allogamous (\u003Ca href=\"#B30\">Punja and Holmes, 2020\u003C/a>). However, vegetative cuttings can lose vigor, since donor (mother or stock) plants can be affected by fungal pathogens and viruses that can reduce their growth and quality (\u003Ca href=\"#B29\">Punja et al., 2019\u003C/a>; \u003Ca href=\"#B28\">Punja, 2021\u003C/a>). However, maintaining donor plants to be used as a source of vegetative cuttings can be time-consuming and space-intensive. Hence, there is interest in using tissue culture methods to propagate cannabis, as it is recognized as a means to potentially increase plant numbers of desired genotypes (micropropagation), and maintain them in a controlled and stable environment (preservation) (\u003Ca href=\"#B21\">Monthony et al., 2021\u003C/a>). In addition, callus production (callogenesis) from explants in tissue culture can potentially be used to increase plant numbers through organogenesis (\u003Ca href=\"#B26\">Page et al., 2021\u003C/a>). However, there are still many challenges remaining in establishing a micropropagation and callusing system for cannabis plants (\u003Ca href=\"#B21\">Monthony et al., 2021\u003C/a>). The quality of source (donor) plants, genotype or strain used, surface sterilization methods, explant type, and tissue culture medium and growth regulators can all influence the success rate of recovery of plantlets in stage 1 of tissue culture (the introduction of explant material for establishment of cultures). Rooting and acclimatization of plantlets are challenging aspects to tissue culture of hemp and cannabis as well. \u003Ca href=\"#B14\">Lata et al. (2009)\u003C/a> reported successful propagation of strain MX-1 of cannabis in tissue culture, but it is not known whether this method can be applied to other genotypes of cannabis. \u003Ca href=\"#B21\">Monthony et al. (2021)\u003C/a> described a procedure for the micropropagation of six cannabis genotypes, but rooting and plantlet recovery were not addressed. The success of a tissue culture method for cannabis is contingent upon obtaining a high frequency of plantlets growing independently in growth media.\u003C/p>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">In this research, we investigated various factors that can influence the recovery of plantlets of cannabis in tissue culture. The objectives of this study were to (1) assess the efficacy of sterilization methods to reduce the frequency of contaminants originating from donor plants; (2) recover and identify the various microbes present as contaminants in tissue cultures; (3) evaluate shoot growth from meristems of five different genotypes; (4) determine the responses of seven different genotypes to shoot growth from nodal explants; (5) evaluate the response of 11 different genotypes to callus development; (6) develop a rooting method for plantlets derived from tissue culture; and (7) evaluate acclimatization responses in different growth substrates (peat, rockwool, and hydroponic system) to achieve a high frequency of plantlet recovery.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ca id=\"h3\" name=\"h3\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Materials and Methods\u003C/h2>\n\u003Ch3 class=\"pt0\">Genotypes\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">The genotypes used were Moby Dick (MBD), Space Queen (SPQ), Copenhagen Kush (CPH), Cheesequake (CHQ), Pennywise (PWE), Girl Scout Cookie (GSC), Death Bubba (DEB), Afghan Kush (AFK), Island Honey (ISH), Pink Kush (PNK), Pure CBD (CBD), and White Rhino (WHR). Various combinations of genotypes were used in experiments, depending on their availability. Donor plants of the various genotypes were grown by a licensed producer according to Health Canada requirements in a controlled-environment room or under commercial greenhouse conditions The growing medium for the donor plants was either pure coco fibers or a coco fiber:vermiculite mix (3:1). The plants in the controlled-environment room were placed under two Sunblaster brand 54-watt 6400 k T5HO lights with a 24-h photoperiod. The plants were watered as needed with a solution of 1 ml/L Sensi Grow Coco pH Perfect A&#x0002B;B (Advanced Nutrients, Los Angeles, CA, Untied States) and 1 ml/L CALiMAGIc (General Hydroponics, Santa Rosa, CA, United States) adjusted to a pH of 5.8&#x02013;6.2 using pH-Down (Advanced Nutrients, Los Angeles, CA, Untied States) (\u003Ca href=\"#B37\">Scott and Punja, 2021\u003C/a>). The plants in the commercial greenhouse were grown according to the standards for the industry and kept under a 16-h photoperiod to maintain vegetative growth. Explants were taken as needed for the tissue culture experiments described.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ch3>Explants\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">The explants tested included meristems, nodal segments, leaves, and petiole tissues. Meristems were dissected from terminal and lateral shoots from donor plants (\u003Ca href=\"#F1\">Figure 1A\u003C/a>). The external tissue was cut away with a scalpel, and two sets of primordial leaves were left to protect the meristem from excessive damage from sterilization. Nodal segments with axillary buds were removed from lateral stems of the plants and trimmed to a 1-cm length (\u003Ca href=\"#F2\">Figure 2A\u003C/a>). Leaves were trimmed from the plants and used for leaf explants in callus induction experiments. They were cut into pieces measuring 0.5 to 1 cm\u003Csup>2\u003C/sup>. Petiole segments from young leaves were cut into 1-cm long pieces for callus induction.\u003C/p>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">FIGURE 1\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g001.jpg\" name=\"figure1\" target=\"_blank\">\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g001.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g001.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g001.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g001.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource type=\"image/jpg\" srcset=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g001.jpg\" media=\"\"> \u003Cimg src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g001.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F1\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 1\u003C/strong>. Various stages of growth of shoots derived from meristems of drug-type cannabis genotypes grown on a multiplication medium (MM) containing activated charcoal. \u003Cstrong>(A)\u003C/strong> Meristem explants placed on an agar medium in a 90-mm Petri dish to show their small size. \u003Cstrong>(B)\u003C/strong> Early shoot growth after 4 weeks in culture from a meristem. \u003Cstrong>(C)\u003C/strong> Shoot growth after 8 weeks. \u003Cstrong>(D,E)\u003C/strong> Shoot growth after 10 weeks from meristems. \u003Cstrong>(F)\u003C/strong> Baby food jars containing meristem explants at different stages of development in a controlled environment growth chamber. A number of different strains are shown. Conditions of incubation are described in the Methods section.\u003C/p>\n\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine mb15\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">FIGURE 2\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g002.jpg\" name=\"figure2\" target=\"_blank\">\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g002.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g002.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g002.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g002.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource type=\"image/jpg\" srcset=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g002.jpg\" media=\"\"> \u003Cimg src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g002.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F2\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 2\u003C/strong>. Stages of growth of shoots derived from nodal segments of drug-type cannabis genotypes grown on MM containing activated charcoal. \u003Cstrong>(A)\u003C/strong> Nodal stem explants placed on an agar medium in a 90-mm Petri dish to show their size. \u003Cstrong>(B)\u003C/strong> Shoot growth after 4 weeks from a nodal stem explant. The rate of growth is greater than that from a meristem. \u003Cstrong>(C)\u003C/strong> Shoot growth after 6 weeks. \u003Cstrong>(D)\u003C/strong> Shoot growth after 8 weeks. \u003Cstrong>(E)\u003C/strong> Shoot growth after 8 weeks of genotype Death Bubba (DEB) from nodal explants. \u003Cstrong>(F)\u003C/strong> Shoot growth after 8 weeks of genotype Pink Kush (PNK) from nodal explants. \u003Cstrong>(G)\u003C/strong> Shoot growth after 8 weeks of genotype White Rhino (WHR) from nodal explants. \u003Cstrong>(H)\u003C/strong> Shoot growth after 8 weeks of genotype Moby Dick (MBD) from nodal explants.\u003C/p>\n\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Ch3>Sterilization\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">The standard sterilization protocol involved placing explants in a stainless steel tea strainer and immersing them in 70% EtOH in a glass beaker for 1 min while stirring with a magnetic stir bar. The explants were transferred to a 10% bleach solution (0.625% NaOCl) with 0.1% Tween 20 for 20 min, followed by three rinses in sterile distilled water, 3 min each. In some of the experiments, the bleach concentration, length of sterilization, and length of rinsing were adjusted according to explant type and source. The explants were blotted dry on sterile filter paper placed in a laminar flow hood and used immediately for tissue culture experiments.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ch3>Media\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">The medium containing Murashige and Skoog basal salts, as described by \u003Ca href=\"#B14\">Lata et al. (2009)\u003C/a>, was used in initial tissue culture experiments. The medium was supplemented with myo-inositol (0.1 g/L) and activated charcoal (1 g/L). The growth regulators added were thidiazuron (TDZ, 1 &#x003BC;M) and naphthaleneacetic acid (NAA, 0.5 &#x003BC;M). This combination of ingredients constitutes a multiplication medium, referred to as MM. All the chemical reagents were from Sigma-Aldrich (St. Louis, MO). The medium was adjusted to pH 6.6 &#x000B1; 0.01 before autoclaving for 20 min at 121&#x000B0;C. Following autoclaving, the pH dropped to &#x0007E;5.8. The medium was dispensed into 220 ml culture jars C1770 with Magenta B caps (Phytotechnology Laboratories&#x000AE;, Lenexa, KS, United States), and each jar received &#x0007E;25 ml of the medium. A single meristem or nodal explant was placed inside each jar. For callus induction, MM without activated charcoal (MM-AC) was used. Each 90-mm Petri dish received &#x0007E;25 ml of the medium onto which four to five leaves or petiole segments were placed.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ch3>Meristem and Nodal Explant Growth\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">For meristems, genotypes Cheesequake, Pure CBD, Moby Dick, Pennywise, and Space Queen were used. Jars containing meristem explants prepared as described above were placed inside a Conviron A1000 growth chamber (Conviron Environments Ltd., Manitoba, Canada) under T5 fluorescent lights with an 18-h photoperiod and a light intensity of 102 &#x003BC;moles m\u003Csup>&#x02212;2\u003C/sup> s\u003Csup>&#x02212;1\u003C/sup> (\u003Ca href=\"#F1\">Figure 1\u003C/a>). The temperature range was 25 &#x000B1; 2&#x000B0;C. The meristems were left in culture for 6 weeks and transferred to fresh MM medium and incubated for another 4 weeks (\u003Ca href=\"#F1\">Figure 1\u003C/a>). At this time, shoot height, number of axillary buds developing, and number of shoots that formed were evaluated (\u003Ca href=\"#T1\">Table 1\u003C/a>). There was a minimum of 10 replicate jars, each containing a meristem for each genotype, and the experiment was conducted three times (\u003Ci>n\u003C/i> = 30) using different sources of explants of the same genotype. The shoots were maintained in culture for a maximum of 3 months by monthly transfer to fresh MM medium.\u003C/p>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">TABLE 1\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-t001.jpg\" name=\"Table1\" target=\"_blank\">\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-t001.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-t001.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-t001.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-t001.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource type=\"image/jpg\" srcset=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-t001.jpg\" media=\"\"> \u003Cimg src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-t001.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"T1\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\n\u003C/a>\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Table 1\u003C/strong>. Comparison of growth of shoots from meristems and nodal explants of five genotypes of drug-type \u003Ci>Cannabis sativa\u003C/i>.\u003C/p>\n\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0 w100pc float_left mt15\">For nodal segments, genotypes BLD, SPQ, MBD, and SWD were used. Jars containing a segment each were incubated under T5 fluorescent lights with an 18-h photoperiod and a light intensity of 102 &#x003BC;moles m\u003Csup>&#x02212;2\u003C/sup> s\u003Csup>&#x02212;1\u003C/sup> and a temperature range of 21&#x02013;27&#x000B0;C for 2 weeks. The shoots were transferred to fresh MM medium and allowed to grow for another 4 weeks (\u003Ca href=\"#F2\">Figure 2\u003C/a>). The percentage of explants surviving, shoot height, number of shoots per explant, number of buds developing, and number of leaves were recorded (\u003Ca href=\"#T1\">Table 1\u003C/a>). For each genotype tested, there was a minimum of 10 replicate jars, each containing a nodal explant, and the experiment was conducted three times (\u003Ci>n\u003C/i> = 30) using different explant sources of the same genotype. To determine if there were differences between the genotypes, the data were analyzed using Statplus version 2.21 and R systems (version 3.3.3). ANOVA was performed followed by Tukey&#x00027;s honestly significant difference (HSD) test to determine significance at \u003Ci>P\u003C/i> \u003Cstrong>&#x0003C; \u003C/strong> 0.05.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ch3>Comparison of DKW and MS Salts\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Two basal salts media, Driver and Kuniyuki Walnut (cat.&#x00023;D190; Phytotechnology Laboratories&#x000AE;, Lenexa, KS, United States) and Murashige and Skoog (cat.&#x00023;M5524; Sigma Aldrich, Saint Louis, MO, United States), were compared for nodal explant growth using genotypes CPH and PWE. The composition of DKW medium, as used in this study, is as follows: 5.22 g/L DKW basal salts, 20 g/L sucrose, 0.1 g/L myo-inositol, 1 ml/L Gamborg B5 vitamins, 0.5 uM NAA, 1 uM TDZ, 1 g/L activated charcoal, and 3 g/L Phytagel. Explants were sterilized as described above but rinsed for 1 min instead of 3 min. They were transferred to either DKW or MM. Twenty jars, each containing a nodal explant, were placed under the lighting conditions described above. The initial height of the nodal explants was measured 4 days after placement on each medium to account for differences between initial explant size. The explants were transferred to the respective fresh media after 2 weeks, and 2 weeks later, the heights of the shoot from the base where it attached to the original explant to the top were measured using ImageJ. The height differences from the start to the end of the experiment represented the shoot height for each explant. The number of leaves on nodal explants on each medium was also assessed (\u003Ca href=\"#F3\">Figure 3\u003C/a>). The experiment was conducted twice. Only data from nodal stem segments that grew were included in the statistical analysis. The shoot height data for CPH on both media were compared by an unpaired two-sample \u003Ci>t\u003C/i>-test. For the data on leaf numbers for CPH and the shoot length data and number of leaves data for PWE, Wilcoxon Rank Sum non-parametric tests were performed as the data did not meet the &#x0201C;normality&#x0201D; assumptions of the unpaired two-sample \u003Ci>t\u003C/i>-test.\u003C/p>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">FIGURE 3\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g003.jpg\" name=\"figure3\" target=\"_blank\">\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g003.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g003.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g003.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g003.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource type=\"image/jpg\" srcset=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g003.jpg\" media=\"\"> \u003Cimg src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g003.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F3\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 3\u003C/strong>. Comparison of shoot growth and leaf number from nodal stem explants of two cannabis genotypes placed on a basal salt medium containing either Driver and Kuniyuki Walnut (DKW) or Murashige and Skoog (MS) salts. \u003Cstrong>(A)\u003C/strong> Shoot length of genotype Copenhagen Kush (CPH). \u003Cstrong>(B)\u003C/strong> Shoot length of genotype Pennywise (PWE). \u003Cstrong>(C)\u003C/strong> Leaf number of genotype Copenhagen Kush (CPH). \u003Cstrong>(D)\u003C/strong> Leaf numbers of genotype Pennywise (PWE). The box of each dataset represents the interquartile range (IQR), which contains the third quartile (Q3&#x02013;top side of the box), the median value of all of the data (the middle line), and the first quartile (Q1&#x02013;bottom side of the box). The bars represent the maximum (Q3 &#x0002B; 1.5&#x0002A;IQR) and minimum (Q1&#x02013;1.5&#x0002A;IQR) of the data. The numbers (\u003Ci>n\u003C/i>) above the bars depict explant numbers used.\u003C/p>\n\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Ch3>Internal Contaminants in Nodal Explants\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">In many nodal explants derived from donor plants used in tissue culture experiments, contamination by fungi, bacteria, and yeasts was frequently observed on the agar medium, in some cases up to 50%. This caused many of the explants to die (\u003Ca href=\"#F4\">Figure 4\u003C/a>). To reduce the level of microbial contamination, the following treatments were assessed by adding them to MM by filter sterilization after autoclaving: (1) the fungicide captan (Maestro 8-DF) was added at 0.01 and 0.02 g/L; (2) streptomycin sulfate was added at 100 mg/L; (3) Plant Preservative Mixture (PPM) (Plant Cell Technology, Washington, DC, United States) was added at 2 mg/L. For all treatments, 10 nodal stem segments of genotype CHQ were used for each of the treatments, and the experiment was conducted twice (\u003Ci>n\u003C/i> = 20). Assessments of the extent of microbial contamination were made after 4 weeks. To assess the effect of fungicides on endophytic contamination, the fungicide Luna (fluopyram) was applied to donor plants of genotypes CPH and PWE at 5 ml/L as a foliar spray until run-off. Plants were grown for 3 weeks before nodal stem segments were collected. Nodal stem segments from treated and control plants were dissected and sterilized with ethanol:bleach as described previously and rinsed with sterile distilled three times for 1 min each. The nodal stem segments were transferred to MM and grown under a 16-h photoperiod for 4 weeks, after which the proportion of jars with microbial contamination was evaluated. A total of 20 explants were included for each treatment group. The data obtained from the CPH genotype met the requirements for a chi-square test, but the data from PWE did not, so Fisher&#x00027;s Exact test was performed. The experiment was conducted once. The sample size was \u003Ci>n\u003C/i> = 20 for each group.\u003C/p>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">FIGURE 4\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g004.jpg\" name=\"figure4\" target=\"_blank\">\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g004.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g004.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g004.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g004.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource type=\"image/jpg\" srcset=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g004.jpg\" media=\"\"> \u003Cimg src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g004.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F4\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 4\u003C/strong>. Range of microbial contaminants emerging from surface-sterilized nodal stem explants at various times after placement on MM containing activated charcoal. \u003Cstrong>(A)\u003C/strong> Early emergence of \u003Ci>Penicillium\u003C/i> species. \u003Cstrong>(B)\u003C/strong> Large colony of \u003Ci>Penicillium\u003C/i> growing from a nodal explant. \u003Cstrong>(C)\u003C/strong> Colony of \u003Ci>Chaetomium\u003C/i> emerging from a nodal explant. \u003Cstrong>(D)\u003C/strong> Large colony of \u003Ci>Penicillium\u003C/i> growing out of a nodal stem explant. \u003Cstrong>(E)\u003C/strong> Extensive \u003Ci>Bacillus\u003C/i> growth emerging from a nodal stem explant. \u003Cstrong>(F)\u003C/strong> Growth of \u003Ci>Pseudomonas\u003C/i> from a stem explant. \u003Cstrong>(G&#x02013;I)\u003C/strong> Death of established shoots from nodal stem segments due to contamination by microbes appearing later during plantlet growth.\u003C/p>\n\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Ch3>Identification of Tissue Culture Contaminants\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Colonies representing the most common contaminants seen in tissue culture were transferred to potato dextrose agar &#x0002B; streptomycin (130 mg/L) for 2 weeks and then to potato dextrose broth placed on a shaker at 125 rpm at room temperature for 7 days. The mycelium was harvested, and DNA was extracted using the DNeasy Plant Mini Kit (cat. No. 69104; QIAGEN, Hilden, Germany). For PCR, the internal transcribed spacer regions (ITS1 and ITS2) as well as the 5.8S gene of ribosomal rDNA were amplified using the universal eukaryotic primers UN-UP18S42 (5&#x02032;-CGTAACAAGGTTTCCGTAGGTGAAC-3&#x02032;) and UN-LO28S576B (5&#x02032;-GTTTCTTTTCCTCCGCTTATTAATATG-3&#x02032;) to produce a DNA template for sequencing. PCR conditions were as follows: initial denaturation at 94&#x000B0;C for 3 min, followed by 40 cycles of denaturation at 94&#x000B0;C for 30 s, annealing at 55&#x000B0;C for 45 s, and extension at 72&#x000B0;C for 2 min, and a final extension at 72&#x000B0;C for 7 min, followed by 4&#x000B0;C hold. PCR products were cut and sent to Eurofins Genomics (Eurofins MWG Operon LLC 2016, Louisville, KY, United States) for sequencing. The resulting sequences were compared with the corresponding ITS1-5.8S-ITS2 sequences from the National Center for Biotechnology Information (NCBI) GenBank database to confirm species identity using only sequence identity values above 99%. Once the identity of the cultured microbes was determined, random samples of nodal explants were obtained from the same donor plants (genotypes CPH and MBD), and 10&#x02013;50 mg of fresh tissue was used for total DNA extraction and PCR following the conditions described above for fungal cultures. For examination of the nodal explants under a scanning electron microscope for potential microbes that could be present in internal tissues, samples were taken from donor plants known to be contaminated based on isolation in culture. They were processed following the procedure described by \u003Ca href=\"#B29\">Punja et al. (2019)\u003C/a>.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ch3>Rooting of Shoots From Nodal Explants\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Genotype Moby Dick was used for root induction. Following the 8-week growth period for nodal segments, shoots measuring 2 cm in height were transferred to MS basal salts medium with the following amendments to induce rooting. The amendments were silver nitrate (40 &#x003BC;M), sodium metasilicate (6 and 9 mg/L), indole-3-butyric acid (5, 12.3, 37, and 42 &#x003BC;M), 2,4-dichlorophenoxyacetic acid (5 &#x003BC;M), and kinetin (1 &#x003BC;M). The shoots were incubated for 4 weeks on media containing these additives and rated for rooting frequency. Each treatment had a minimum of 10 explants, and the experiment was repeated three times (\u003Ci>n\u003C/i> = 30). The data were analyzed using Statplus version 2.21 and R systems (version 3.3.3). ANOVA was performed followed by Tukey&#x00027;s HSD test to determine significance at \u003Ci>P\u003C/i> &#x0003C; 0.05.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ch3>Acclimatization for Plantlet Recovery\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Shoots of genotype Moby Dick derived from either meristems or nodal explants that had formed roots were selected at random and transferred to one of the following growing substrates: peat plugs (Jiffy-7&#x000AE; peat pellets), rockwool cubes (2.5 &#x000D7; 2.5 &#x000D7; 3.8 cm, Grodan) or placed in a Turboklone (T-24 turbo-mini) (\u003Ca href=\"https://turboklone.com\">https://turboklone.com\u003C/a>) containing a hydroponic nutrient solution with 1 ml/L of pH Perfect&#x000AE; Sensi Grow A&#x00026;B and CALiMAGIc (General Hydroponics, Santa Rosa, CA, United States) and 0.25 ml/L of Rapid Start Rooting Enhancer (General Hydroponics, Santa Rosa, CA, United States) &#x0007E;pH 5.8. Every 3 days, a nutrient mixture (without Rapid Start) was added to top up the system. The peat plugs/rockwool cubes were soaked for a minimum of 20 min in the same nutrient solution used for hydroponic growth. The plugs containing shoots were then placed in a sterilized tray (28 &#x000D7; 56 cm) and covered with a plastic dome (\u003Ca href=\"http://www.jiffypot.com/\">http://www.jiffypot.com/\u003C/a>). The domes were misted with water every 2 days, and the vents were opened halfway after 7 days and fully opened after 9 days. Domes were removed 2 weeks post transfer. The percentage of survival of plantlets was assessed 4 weeks after transfer. Each treatment had a minimum of 10 replicates, and the experiment was conducted twice. The data were analyzed using Statplus version 2.21 and R systems (version 3.3.3). ANOVA was performed followed by Tukey&#x00027;s HSD test to determine significance at \u003Ci>P\u003C/i> &#x0003C; 0.05.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ch3>Callus Growth\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">Genotypes Girl Scout Cookie, Space Queen, Moby Dick, and Pennywise were tested. After sterilization, leaf and petiole explants were placed on MM-AC. Petri dishes with leaf and petiole explants were kept on wire rack shelves under ambient conditions (temperature range of 21&#x02013;25&#x000B0;C). One-half of the dishes were wrapped in aluminum foil to exclude light, and the remainder was left under the same conditions of supplemental lighting as for the nodal explants. Callus development was first observed after 4 weeks. Dishes were incubated for 6&#x02013;10 weeks before measurements were taken. The percentage of explants that developed callus as well as the surface area of callus was measured. For the latter, an Alvin TD 1204 Circle Master Template (Alvin &#x00026; Company, Bloomfield, CT, USA) was used to estimate the circle corresponding to the size that matched the callus, and the measurement was converted to cm\u003Csup>2\u003C/sup>.\u003C/p>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">In a subsequent experiment, the following eight genotypes were compared: AFK, CPH, DEB, ISH, PWE, PNK, CBD, and WHR. Leaf segments from greenhouse-grown plants were sterilized in the bleach solution for 15 min and rinsed 3 times for 1 min in sterile ddH\u003Csub>2\u003C/sub>O then placed on MM-AC. The dishes were wrapped in Al foil and incubated at 21&#x02013;23&#x000B0;C for a maximum of 8 weeks. The callus area was measured using the &#x0201C;freehand&#x0201D; tool in ImageJ from photos taken of the Petri dishes that included a ruler for measurement. If the data did not meet the assumptions of parametric tests (i.e., ANOVA, unpaired two-sample \u003Ci>t\u003C/i>-test, etc.), then non-parametric tests were used (i.e., Welch&#x00027;s ANOVA, and Wilcoxon Rank Sum test). If the data met the assumptions of the ANOVA test, then they were used followed \u003Ci>post-hoc\u003C/i> by Tukey&#x00027;s HSD test.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ca id=\"h4\" name=\"h4\">\u003C/a>\u003Ch2>Results\u003C/h2>\n\u003Ch3 class=\"pt0\">Shoot Growth From Meristem and Nodal Explants\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb15\">Placement of meristems on MM medium (with 1% activated charcoal) resulted in shoot growth after 4 weeks in culture (\u003Ca href=\"#F1\">Figure 1B\u003C/a>), which continued to elongate after 8&#x02013;10 weeks (\u003Ca href=\"#F1\">Figures 1C,D\u003C/a>). Baby food jars containing meristem explants at different stages of development were placed in a controlled environment growth chamber to allow for a comparison of growth of different genotypes (\u003Ca href=\"#F1\">Figure 1E\u003C/a>). Meristems of five different cannabis genotypes were assessed for their shoot regeneration response. After 10 weeks of growth, shoot height, number of axillary buds, and number of axillary shoots were measured (\u003Ca href=\"#T1\">Table 1\u003C/a>). The genotype with the greatest shoot height value was MBD, with an average height of 4.5 cm, which was significantly greater compared with genotypes CHQ, SPQ and PWE (\u003Ci>P\u003C/i> &#x0003C; 0.001). The average shoot growth across genotypes was 2.92 cm (\u003Ca href=\"#T1\">Table 1\u003C/a>). The number of axillary buds present on the shoots from meristems ranged from 8.5 for CBD to 3.4 for SPQ. The number of axillary buds produced per plantlet was not significantly different (\u003Ci>P\u003C/i> &#x0003E; 0.05) among CBD, CHQ, MBD, and PWE. SPQ had the lowest number of buds compared with the other genotypes (\u003Ci>P\u003C/i> &#x0003C; 0.05). Across genotypes, plantlets from meristems produced 6.38 axillary buds on average and 1.4 shoots (\u003Ca href=\"#T1\">Table 1\u003C/a>). Genotype SPQ showed poor growth for all parameters compared with the four other genotypes. While CBD plantlets were generally shorter, they had a higher number of buds that resulted in a bushier appearance. Genotypes MBD and PWE had similar shoot growth, but MBD was significantly taller than PWE (\u003Ca href=\"#T1\">Table 1\u003C/a>). Genotype SPQ generally showed the poorest growth among all the genotypes.\u003C/p>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Measurements of height, number of axillary buds, and number of axillary shoots were obtained from nodal stem explants after 6 weeks (\u003Ca href=\"#T1\">Table 1\u003C/a>). The genotypes tested were BLD, SWD, MBD, and SPQ. The average shoot height ranged from 1.5 to 4.9 cm for SPQ and BLD, respectively (\u003Ca href=\"#T1\">Table 1\u003C/a>). BLD and SWD were significantly (\u003Ci>P\u003C/i> &#x0003C; 0.001) taller than MBD and SPQ at 4.9 and 3.8 cm, respectively. The average height across genotypes after 4 weeks was 3.075 cm. The average number of axillary buds across strains was 2.98, and the average number of shoots across genotypes was 0.3 (\u003Ca href=\"#T1\">Table 1\u003C/a>).\u003C/p>\n\u003Ch3>Comparison of DKW and MS Salts\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Shoot length and leaf numbers from nodal stem segments were evaluated for genotypes CPH and PWE after 4 weeks of growth on DKW or MM salts (\u003Ca href=\"#F3\">Figure 3\u003C/a>). For CPH, 78.4% of explants on DKW and 89.2% on MM basal salts grew and were included in a statistical comparison by an unpaired two-sample \u003Ci>t\u003C/i>-test. For PWE, 70% of explants on DKW and 77.5% on MM grew and were included in a statistical comparison by the Wilcoxon Rank Sum test. The data showed that there was a significant difference between DKW and MS basal salts in shoot length for CPH (\u003Ci>p\u003C/i> = 0.02947; \u003Ca href=\"#F3\">Figure 3A\u003C/a>). The average CPH shoot length on DKW and MM was 6.44 and 8.63 mm, respectively. In comparing shoot length for PWE on DKW and MM, there was no significant difference (\u003Ci>p\u003C/i> = 0.08354). The average PWE shoot length on DKW and MS was 7.66 and 9.2 mm, respectively (\u003Ca href=\"#F3\">Figure 3B\u003C/a>). For CPH leaf number, there was no difference between DKW and MM (\u003Ci>p\u003C/i> = 0.6882; \u003Ca href=\"#F3\">Figure 3C\u003C/a>). The average number of leaves on DKW and MM was 3.31 and 3.12, respectively. For PWE leaf number, there was a significant difference between DKW and MM (\u003Ci>p\u003C/i> = 0.04468; \u003Ca href=\"#F3\">Figure 3D\u003C/a>). The average number of leaves on DKW and MM was 4.13 and 5.64, respectively.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ch3>Internal Contaminants and Alternative Sterilization Methods\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">In general, an average of 50% of nodal stem explants were found to be contaminated by microbes (a range of 10 to 80%), despite the rigorous sterilization methods used, and these had to be discarded. These contaminants included fungi, bacteria, and yeasts (\u003Ca href=\"#F4\">Figure 4\u003C/a>). Due to the high frequency of microbial contaminants from nodal stem explants when grown in a tissue culture medium, the addition of fungicides and antibiotics was assessed. The addition of a fungicide (captan) at 0.01 and 0.02 g/L to the tissue culture medium post-sterilization did not significantly reduce the contamination frequency on genotype CHQ (\u003Ci>P\u003C/i> = 0.05). The addition of streptomycin sulfate at 100 mg/L caused stunting and chlorosis of the tissue culture shoots (data not shown). Plant preservative mixture (PPM) at 2 ml/L appeared to delay the onset of contamination, but microbial growth would appear 1 to 3 weeks later. Nodal stem explants sterilized with 5% PPM &#x0002B; 3x basal salt solution or 70% EtOH and 10% bleach &#x0002B;0.1% Tween 20 showed no differences in microbial contamination on genotype CPH (60.5 vs. 60%, chi-square test \u003Ci>p\u003C/i> &#x0003E; 0.05). For CPH donor plants treated with Luna fungicide, a chi-square test was significant at \u003Ci>p\u003C/i> &#x0003C; 0.05, which suggests that fungicide treatment of donor plants could reduce the endophytic contamination observed in tissue culture. The CPH control group showed 88.2% contamination, and the Luna treatment group showed 31.8% contamination. However, the PWE plants treated with Luna did not differ significantly from the untreated control as overall contamination rates were much lower (12.5 vs. 16.7%, Fisher&#x00027;s exact test \u003Ci>p\u003C/i> &#x0003E; 0.05). These results indicate that genotypes may differ in terms of the extent of microbial contamination.\u003C/p>\n\u003Ch3>Identification of Tissue Culture Contaminants\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">The contaminants observed growing on agar media consisted of bacteria, which were identified to genus level as \u003Ci>Bacillus\u003C/i> and \u003Ci>Pseudomonas\u003C/i>, yeasts that were not identified, and many fungi. In an attempt to better categorize the fungal contaminants, which represented the majority of contaminants present in nodal stem tissues, a PCR-based method was utilized. Cultures of 11 fungi recovered from nodal explants each produced a band size of about 650 bp after PCR. After sequencing and comparing the results in BlastN, a range of fungi were identified. This was followed by using naturally contaminated nodal explants, in which the frequency of detection of fungal contamination was 16/19 samples (\u003Ca href=\"#F5\">Figures 5A,B\u003C/a>). The plant DNA band was at 750 bp, as the universal primers used amplified both fungal and plant DNA. The genus and species of fungi present in cannabis stem tissues are shown in \u003Ca href=\"#F5\">Figure 5\u003C/a>. The PCR test could detect fungal contaminants present at DNA concentrations of 1 ng/ul (qPCR data not shown). Sections of stem segments when plated onto potato dextrose agar yielded colonies of \u003Ci>Penicillium\u003C/i> that emerged directly from the pith tissues (\u003Ca href=\"#F5\">Figure 5C\u003C/a>). When pith tissues were examined under a scanning electron microscope (\u003Ca href=\"#F5\">Figure 5D\u003C/a>), a close-up showed that fungal spores could be seen growing in and around pith cells (\u003Ca href=\"#F5\">Figures 5E,F\u003C/a>).\u003C/p>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">FIGURE 5\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g005.jpg\" name=\"figure5\" target=\"_blank\">\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g005.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g005.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g005.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g005.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource type=\"image/jpg\" srcset=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g005.jpg\" media=\"\"> \u003Cimg src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g005.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F5\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 5\u003C/strong>. (arrow).\u003C/p>\n\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"clear\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Ch3>Rooting of Shoots From Nodal Explants\u003C/h3>\n\u003Cp class=\"mb0\">Representative plantlets from sodium metasilicate treatments of 0, 6, and 9 mg/L are shown in \u003Ca href=\"#F6\">Figures 6A&#x02013;C\u003C/a>. The addition of sodium metasilicate caused visible differences in plant growth and leaf morphology, recorded on a scale of 1&#x02013;3, as shown in \u003Ca href=\"#F6\">Figures 6D&#x02013;F\u003C/a>. A rating of 1 shows thin curled leaves, while a rating of 3 shows dark green, flat, and toothed margins. The addition of sodium metasilicate at 6 mg/L significantly (\u003Ci>p\u003C/i> &#x0003C; 0.05) improved the leaf morphology rating compared with MM and MM &#x0002B; Na\u003Csub>2\u003C/sub>SiO\u003Csub>3\u003C/sub> at 9 mg/L according to Tukey&#x00027;s HSD test (\u003Ca href=\"#F6\">Figure 6G\u003C/a>). On the MM &#x0002B; 6 mg/L Na\u003Csub>2\u003C/sub>SiO\u003Csub>3\u003C/sub>, the leaf morphology rating was comparable with the leaf rating of plants grown on the MM without Na\u003Csub>2\u003C/sub>SiO\u003Csub>3\u003C/sub>. The addition of Na\u003Csub>2\u003C/sub>SiO\u003Csub>3\u003C/sub> at 6 mg/L produced the greatest proportion of rooted plantlets according to Tukey&#x00027;s HSD test (\u003Ci>p\u003C/i> &#x0003C; 0.05; \u003Ca href=\"#F6\">Figure 6H\u003C/a>). The proportion of rooted plants was 0.4 (40%) for MM &#x0002B; Na\u003Csub>2\u003C/sub>SiO\u003Csub>3\u003C/sub> at 6 mg/L compared with 0.1, 0.1, and 0.2 for treatments MM, MM &#x0002B; 9 mg/L Na\u003Csub>2\u003C/sub>SiO\u003Csub>3\u003C/sub>, and MM without phytohormones (MMC). The addition of sodium metasilicate did not significantly affect any of the growth parameters measured for nodal stem segments or meristems: height, number of buds, and number of shoots (data not shown).\u003C/p>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">FIGURE 6\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g006.jpg\" name=\"figure6\" target=\"_blank\">\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g006.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g006.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g006.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g006.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource type=\"image/jpg\" srcset=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g006.jpg\" media=\"\"> \u003Cimg src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g006.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F6\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 6\u003C/strong>. KIN and 2,4-D were tested at 1 and 5 &#x003BC;M, respectively, alone and in combination. Neither hormone alone or in combination performed significantly better than the MM (data not shown). The combination of KIN and 2,4-D produced an average of 63% rooted plants, while the MM produced an average of 44% rooted plants. The MMC was used as the basal medium in the indole-3-butyric acid experiments. IBA was tested at 5, 12.3, 37, and 42 &#x003BC;M. While there was a trend toward decreased rooting as more IBA was added, the only statistically significant difference \u003Ci>(p\u003C/i> &#x0003C; 0.05 by Tukey&#x00027;s HSD test) was between 5 and 42 &#x003BC;M (\u003Ca href=\"#F7\">Figure 7A\u003C/a>). The MM (listed as C in \u003Ca href=\"#F7\">Figure 7A\u003C/a>) was not significantly different from any of the IBA concentrations; however, it averaged 44% rooted plants, while IBA at 5 &#x003BC;M produced 83% rooting.\u003C/p>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"DottedLine\">\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"Imageheaders\">FIGURE 7\u003C/div>\n\u003Cdiv class=\"FigureDesc\">\n\u003Ca href=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g007.jpg\" name=\"figure7\" target=\"_blank\">\n\n  \u003Cpicture>\n    \u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=480&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g007.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 563px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=370&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g007.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1024px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=290&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g007.jpg\" media=\"(max-width: 1441px)\">\u003Csource type=\"image/webp\" srcset=\"https://images-provider.frontiersin.org/api/ipx/w=410&f=webp/https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g007.jpg\" media=\"\">\u003Csource type=\"image/jpg\" srcset=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g007.jpg\" media=\"\"> \u003Cimg src=\"https://www.frontiersin.org/files/Articles/732344/fpls-12-732344-HTML-r1/image_m/fpls-12-732344-g007.jpg\" alt=\"www.frontiersin.org\" id=\"F7\" loading=\"lazy\">\n  \u003C/picture>\n\u003C/a>\n\u003Cp>\u003Cstrong>Figure 7\u003C/strong>. The effect of additives to the MS salts medium on root development in plantlets of genotype MBD derived from nodal stem segments after 4 weeks of growth.
